It seems that many numbers eventually go to a prime or square of prime and fail, but some numbers map to a fixed point. We need to characterize all a_1 that lead to an infinite sequence. We can try to characterize the dynamics.

Observation: For any N with at least three proper divisors, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is either N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor (if N has at least two distinct prime factors) or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and the exponent >=2. In general, the three largest proper divisors are N/d_2, N/d_3, N/d_4, where d_2,d_3,d_4 are the three smallest divisors >1.

Let’s denote N = 6k, where k is a positive integer. Then N has divisors: 1,2,3,6, and also divisors that come from factors of k. The smallest divisors >1: 2 and 3 are always there. The third smallest divisor d_4 is the smallest divisor of N that is greater than 3. That could be:

This is reminiscent of a "survival" set in the Collatz-like problem. We need to characterize it.

Observation: The recurrence S(N) is monotonic? Not exactly, but we can analyze the behavior for numbers that are multiples of 6. Let's denote f(N) = S(N). We have:
- If N is divisible by 12, then f(N) = 13N/12.
- If N is divisible by 30 but not by 12, then f(N) = 31N/30.
- If N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5, then f(N) = N (fixed point).

Now, what about numbers that map to these fixed points? For example, 72 maps to 78 (fixed). 72 is multiple of 6? 72/6=12, k=12 even, so it is in case A (divisible by 12). We need to determine all a_1 for which the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This is a classification problem. Often in such problems, the answer is that a_1 must be of the form 2 * 3^k * p where p is a prime >3, or something like that. Let's analyze the dynamics more systematically. Let's denote N = 6k. Then f(N) = 
- If k even: f(N) = 13N/12 = 13*6k/12 = 13k/2? Actually 13N/12 = 13*6k/12 = 13k/2. Since k even, 13k/2 integer.
- If k odd and 5∤k: f(N) = N (fixed)
- If k odd and 5|k: f(N) = 31N/30 = 31*6k/30 = 31k/5. Since k multiple of 5, 31k/5 integer.

So the function f on multiples of 6 is given by: f(6k) = 
- if k even: 13k/2
- if k odd and 5∤k: 6k
- if k odd and 5|k: 31k/5

But note that the result may not be a multiple of 6. For instance, if k even, 13k/2 may not be a multiple of 6. So we need to consider the behavior for numbers that are not multiples of 6 as well.

The condition for the sequence to be infinite is that every term has at least 3 proper divisors. So we need to characterize the set of numbers that are "safe". It might be easier to work with the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point, and then show that any number that does not eventually reach a fixed point must hit a prime or square of prime. This is a typical approach: we need to determine all initial values a_1 for which the sequence never hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. Since the recurrence is deterministic, we can try to show that the only numbers that can be in the survival set are those that are multiples of 6 and also satisfy that when repeatedly applying f, we always get multiples of 6? Now, we need to verify that for any such N, the sequence indeed consists of positive integers each having at least three proper divisors. We already argued that if N=12^a * F, then f(N)=13 * 12^{a-1} * F for a≥1, and eventually we get to 13^b * F? Actually careful: If N=12^a * F, then f(N)=13 * 12^{a-1} * F. For F fixed, 13^a * F = 13^a * 6k = 6*(13^a k). Since k is coprime to 10, and 13 is coprime to 10, 13^a k is also coprime to 10. So 13^a * F is a fixed point. So the orbit goes: N -> 13 * 12^{a-1} F -> 13^2 * 12^{a-2} F -> ... -> 13^a * F, which is a fixed point. So the sequence becomes constant after a steps. Thus, all terms are defined and have at least three proper divisors because the fixed points have that property, and the intermediate terms are multiples of 12 (or at least have many divisors). So indeed, any such N works.

Now, we need to prove that if a_1 does not satisfy this condition, then the sequence fails. That is, if a_1 is not of the form 12^a * F with F a fixed point, then at some point a_n is prime or a square of a prime (or 1). This requires a thorough analysis.

Let's attempt to prove the necessity. Suppose a_1 is a positive integer such that the sequence a_n exists for all n (i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors). We need to show that a_1 must be in the described set.

Observation: The function f(N) = S(N) is defined for all N≥2 with at least 3 proper divisors. But if N is prime or square of prime, it's not defined, so we avoid those. So the orbit stays within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. We can try to understand the structure of such numbers.

Let's denote N = a_n. Then a_{n+1} = f(N). We can examine the possible values.

Let's construct the proof:

**Solution:**

Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\), and it is required that each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.

We first show that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

- If \(a_1\) is odd, then all its proper divisors are odd. Let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor, so \(p\ge3\). The largest proper divisor is \(a_1/p\), which is at most \(a_1/3\). The second largest proper divisor is at most \(a_1/3\) as well (since the next smallest divisor is at least \(p\), and the largest divisor less than \(a_1/p\) is at most \(a_1/p^2\) or something). Actually we need a careful bound: For odd \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, we claim \(f(N)<N\). Indeed, the three largest proper divisors are all \(\le N/3\)? Not exactly; the largest is \(N/p\), and the second largest is at most \(N/3\) because the smallest divisor >1 is \(p\), and the next largest divisor is \(N/q\) with \(q\ge p\), so \(N/q \le N/p\). So the sum of the three largest is at most \(N/p+N/p+N/p = 3N/p \le N\) (since \(p\ge3\)). Equality would require \(p=3\) and all three largest divisors to be \(N/3\), but then \(N\) would have only two proper divisors? For \(N=27\), the three largest are \(9,3,1\) sum \(13<27\). So strict inequality holds. Thus \(f(N)<N\) for odd \(N\). Therefore, if \(a_1\) is odd, the sequence strictly decreases, and since it consists of positive integers, it eventually reaches a number \(\le5\). None of \(1,2,3,4,5\) have three proper divisors, so the sequence would break. Hence \(a_1\) cannot be odd.

- Now suppose \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\). Write \(a_1=2^a m\) with \(m\) odd and \(a\ge1\). Since \(3\nmid a_1\), we have \(3\nmid m\). If \(a=1\), then \(a_1=2m\). The proper divisors of \(2m\) include all divisors of \(m\) and all numbers \(2d\) with \(d\mid m\) and \(d<m\). The largest proper divisor is \(m\). The second largest proper divisor is either \(2\cdot(\text{largest proper divisor of }m)\) or some divisor of \(m\) that is larger than that. Since the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\) is at least \(5\), we have \(m/p\le m/5\) for some prime \(p\ge5\). Then the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most \(m+2(m/5)+m/5 = \frac{8}{5}m < 2m = a_1\). If \(a\ge2\), then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\). Hence \(f(a_1)\le \frac{3}{4}a_1+\frac{1}{5}a_1 = \frac{19}{20}a_1 < a_1\). Thus again \(f(a_1)<a_1\). Consequently, the sequence would strictly decrease, eventually hitting a number \(\le5\) and failing. Therefore, \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\).

Combining, \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\). Write \(a_1=6k\), where \(k\) is a positive integer. For the sequence to be well‑defined, each term must have at least three proper divisors. Now, we can write the solution as:

**Solution:**

Let \(S(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=S(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\), and each term must have at least three proper divisors.

We first show that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

- If \(a_1\) is odd, then all its proper divisors are odd. Let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor, so \(p\ge3\). The largest proper divisor is \(a_1/p\le a_1/3\). The second largest is at most \(a_1/p\) as well (since the next smallest divisor is at least \(p\)). Hence \(S(a_1)\le 3a_1/3 = a_1\). Moreover, equality would force \(p=3\) and all three largest divisors to be \(a_1/3\), which would imply that \(a_1\) has only two proper divisors, a contradiction. Thus \(S(a_1)<a_1\). Consequently, the sequence would strictly decrease, eventually reaching a number \(\le5\), none of which have three proper divisors. Hence \(a_1\) cannot be odd.

- Now suppose \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\). Write \(a_1=2^a m\) with \(m\) odd and \(a\ge1\). Since \(3\nmid a_1\), we have \(3\nmid m\). If \(a=1\), then \(a_1=2m\). The proper divisors of \(2m\) include all divisors of \(m\) and all numbers \(2d\) with \(d\mid m\), \(d<m\). The largest is \(m\). The next largest is at most \(2\cdot(m/5)\) if \(p\ge5\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\) (because the largest proper divisor of \(m\) is \(m/p\) and \(p\ge5\)). Hence \(S(2m)\le m+2(m/5)+m/5 = \frac{8}{5}m < 2m = a_1\). If \(a\ge2\), then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\). The three largest proper divisors are \(a_1/2\), \(a_1/4\), and at most \(a_1/5\) (since the smallest divisor after \(2\) and \(4\) is at least \(5\)). Therefore \(S(a_1)\le \frac{3}{4}a_1+\frac{1}{5}a_1 = \frac{19}{20}a_1 < a_1\). Thus again \(S(a_1)<a_1\), and the sequence would decrease, eventually failing. Hence \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\).

Combining, \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\). Write \(a_1=6k\), where \(k\) is a positive integer.

Now analyse the recurrence on the quotient \(k_n = a_n/6\) (if it remains integer). For a multiple of \(6\) we have:

- If \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\), then the three largest proper divisors of \(6k\) are \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(k\), so \(S(6k)=6k\). Hence \(k_{n+1}=k_n\), and the sequence becomes constant from that point.

- If \(k\) is odd and \(5\mid k\), then the three largest proper divisors are \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(6k/5\), so \(S(6k)=31k/5\). Then \(k_{n+1}=31k/30\). Since \(k\) is odd, \(k/5\) is odd, so \(31k/30\) is not an integer; therefore \(a_{n+1}\) is not a multiple of \(6\). As shown above, any term that is not a multiple of \(6\) leads to a strictly decreasing orbit that eventually fails.

- If \(k\) is even, then the three largest proper divisors are \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(3k/2\), so \(S(6k)=13k/2\). Hence \(k_{n+1}=13k/12\). For this to be an integer we need \(k\) to be divisible by \(12\). Thus if \(k\) is even but not a multiple of \(12\), then \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and the sequence fails.

Therefore, for the sequence to be well‑defined for all \(n\), the integer \(k = a_1/6\) must either be odd and not divisible by \(5\), or be a multiple of \(12\). In the first case we already have a fixed point; in the second case we set \(k = 12^a \cdot t\) where \(a\ge1\) and \(t\) is not divisible by \(12\). Repeating the argument we obtain that the process can continue only if \(t\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\). Consequently, \(k\) must be of the form \(k = 12^a \cdot m\) with \(a\ge0\) and \(m\) odd, \(5\nmid m\). This is equivalent to the conditions on the prime exponents of \(a_1\): writing \(a_1 = 2^{\alpha}3^{\beta}M\) with \(M\) coprime to \(2,3,5\), we must have \(\alpha\) odd, \(\beta\ge (\alpha+1)/2\), and \(5\nmid a_1\). Conversely, if these conditions hold, then with \(a = (\alpha-1)/2\) we have \(k = 2^{2a}3^{\beta-1}M = 12^a \cdot (3^{\beta-1-a}M)\). The factor \(3^{\beta-1-a}M\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\) (since \(\beta-1\ge a\) and \(M\) has no factor \(5\)). Thus \(k = 12^a \cdot m\) with \(m\) odd and \(5\nmid m\). Starting from such an \(a_1\) the sequence goes: \(a_1 = 6\cdot12^a\cdot m\), then \(a_2 = 6\cdot12^{a-1}\cdot13m\), …, \(a_a = 6\cdot13^{a-1}\cdot12m\), \(a_{a+1} = 6\cdot13^a\cdot m\). Since \(m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\), \(a_{a+1}\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(k_{a+1} = 13^a m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\); hence \(a_{a+1}\) is a fixed point of the recurrence, and all later terms equal \(a_{a+1}\). Every term is composite and has at least three proper divisors (the fixed point itself has at least three because \(m\ge1\) and \(13^a m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\); if \(m=1\) then \(a_{a+1}=6\cdot13^a\) has proper divisors \(2,3,6\) for \(a=0\), and for \(a>0\) it has many divisors). Therefore the sequence exists for all \(n\).

Thus the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly the positive integers \(N\) that satisfy:
- \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\);
- \(5\nmid N\);
- the exponent of \(2\) in \(N\) is odd;
- the exponent of \(3\) in \(N\) is at least \(\frac{(\text{exponent of }2)+1}{2}\).

In other words, all numbers of the form \(N = 2^{\alpha}3^{\beta}M\) where \(\alpha\) is odd, \(\beta\ge\frac{\alpha+1}{2}\), and \(M\) is any positive integer coprime to \(2,3,5\).

Now, we need to output in XML.

We should write the solution clearly, with proper mathematical notation.

Let's write the proof in a structured way.

We'll produce the solution.</think><solution>
Let \(S(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\).  The sequence \((a_n)\) satisfies \(a_{n+1}=S(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\) and each term must have at least three proper divisors.

We first show that \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

*If \(a_1\) is odd.*  All its proper divisors are odd.  Let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor, so \(p\ge3\).  The largest proper divisor is \(a_1/p\le a_1/3\).  The second largest is at most \(a_1/p\) as well (because the next smallest divisor is at least \(p\)).  Hence \(S(a_1)\le 3a_1/3 = a_1\).  Equality would force \(p=3\) and all three largest divisors to be \(a_1/3\), which would imply that \(a_1\) has only two proper divisors – a contradiction.  Thus \(S(a_1)<a_1\).  The sequence would then strictly decrease, eventually reaching a number \(\le5\).  None of \(1,2,3,4,5\) have three proper divisors, so the sequence would break.  Hence \(a_1\) cannot be odd.

*If \(a_1\) is even but not divisible by \(3\).*  Write \(a_1=2^a m\) with \(m\) odd and \(a\ge1\).  Since \(3\nmid a_1\), we have \(3\nmid m\).  If \(a=1\) then \(a_1=2m\).  The proper divisors of \(2m\) are all divisors of \(m\) together with all numbers \(2d\) where \(d\mid m\) and \(d<m\).  The largest is \(m\).  The next largest is at most \(2\cdot(m/5)\) because the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\) is at least \(5\) (it cannot be \(3\)).  Hence \(S(2m)\le m+2(m/5)+m/5 = \frac85m < 2m = a_1\).  If \(a\ge2\) then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\).  The three largest proper divisors are \(a_1/2\), \(a_1/4\), and at most \(a_1/5\) (since the smallest divisor after \(2\) and \(4\) is at least \(5\)).  Thus \(S(a_1)\le \frac34a_1+\frac15a_1 = \frac{19}{20}a_1 < a_1\).  Again the sequence would strictly decrease and eventually fail.  Therefore \(a_1\) must be divisible by \(3\).

Combining, \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\).  Write \(a_1=6k\) with \(k\in\mathbb{N}\).

Now analyse the recurrence on the quotient \(k_n = a_n/6\) (if it remains integer).  For a multiple of \(6\) we have:

*If \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\).*  The divisors of \(6k\) include \(k\), \(2k\), \(3k\) (since \(k\mid 6k\), \(2k\mid 6k\) and \(3k\mid 6k\)).  No divisor lies strictly between \(k\) and \(2k\) because the smallest prime factor of \(k\) is at least \(3\) and \(k\) is not divisible by \(5\), so the quotient \(6k/(2\cdot3\cdot d)\) with \(d\mid k\) is at most \(k\).  Hence the three largest proper divisors are \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(k\), giving \(S(6k)=6k\).  Thus \(k_{n+1}=k_n\) and the sequence becomes constant from that point.

*If \(k\) is odd and \(5\mid k\).*  Write \(k=5t\) with \(t\) odd.  The numbers \(3k=15t\), \(2k=10t\) and \(6k/5=6t\) are divisors.  Since \(6t\) lies between \(t\) and \(2t\) (and \(t\) is odd, \(2t\) is even), the three largest proper divisors are \(15t\), \(10t\) and \(6t\).  Hence \(S(6k)=31k/5\).  Then \(k_{n+1}=31k/30\) is not an integer because \(k\) is odd and \(30\) is even; consequently \(a_{n+1}\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  As shown above, any term that is not a multiple of \(6\) leads to a strictly decreasing orbit that eventually fails.

*If \(k\) is even.*  Then \(6k\) is divisible by \(12\).  The divisors include \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(3k/2\) (since \(4\mid6k\)).  No other divisor lies between \(2k\) and \(3k\) or between \(3k/2\) and \(2k\) (the next smallest divisor after \(2\) and \(3\) is at least \(4\), and the candidate \(6k/5\) is smaller than \(3k/2\) because \(6/5<3/2\)).  Hence the three largest proper divisors are \(3k\), \(2k\) and \(3k/2\), giving \(S(6k)=13k/2\).  Then \(k_{n+1}=13k/12\).  For this to be an integer we need \(k\) to be divisible by \(12\).  If \(k\) is even but not a multiple of \(12\), then \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and the sequence fails.

Therefore, for the sequence to be defined for all \(n\), the integer \(k=a_1/6\) must either be odd and not divisible by \(5\), or be a multiple of \(12\).  In the first case we already have a fixed point.  In the second case we repeat the argument: let \(k=12^a\cdot t\) where \(a\ge1\) and \(t\) is not divisible by \(12\).  Then after \(a\) steps we obtain \(k_{a+1}=13^a\cdot t\).  For the sequence to continue, \(t\) must be odd and not divisible by \(5\) (otherwise the next term would not be a multiple of \(6\) and the process fails).  Hence \(k\) must be of the form \(k=12^a\cdot m\) with \(a\ge0\) and \(m\) odd, \(5\nmid m\).  Equivalently, writing \(a_1=2^{\alpha}3^{\beta}M\) with \(M\) coprime to \(2,3,5\), we have \(\alpha\) odd, \(\beta\ge\frac{\alpha+1}{2}\) and \(5\nmid a_1\).

Conversely, if these conditions hold, set \(a=(\alpha-1)/2\) (so \(a\ge0\) because \(\alpha\) is odd).  Then \(k=2^{\alpha-1}3^{\beta-1}M = 12^a\cdot(3^{\beta-1-a}M)\).  The factor \(3^{\beta-1-a}M\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\) (because \(\beta-1\ge a\) and \(M\) has no factor \(5\)).  Thus \(k=12^a\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd and \(5\nmid m\).  Starting from such an \(a_1\) we have:
\[
a_1 = 6\cdot12^a\cdot m,\quad
a_2 = 6\cdot12^{a-1}\cdot13m,\quad\dots,
a_a = 6\cdot13^{a-1}\cdot12m,\quad
a_{a+1} = 6\cdot13^a\cdot m.
\]
Since \(m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\), the number \(a_{a+1}\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(k_{a+1}=13^a m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\); therefore \(a_{a+1}\) is a fixed point of the recurrence, and all later terms equal \(a_{a+1}\).  Every term is composite and has at least three proper divisors (the fixed point itself has at least three because it is a multiple of \(6\) and \(13^a m\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\); for example, if \(m=1\) then \(a_{a+1}=6\cdot13^a\) has proper divisors \(2,3,6\) when \(a=0\), and for \(a>0\) it has many divisors).  Hence the sequence exists for all \(n\).

Thus the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly the positive integers \(N\) satisfying:
- \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\);
- \(5\nmid N\);
- the exponent of \(2\) in \(N\) is odd;
- the exponent of \(3\) in \(N\) is at least \(\frac{(\text{exponent of }2)+1}{2}\).

In other words, all numbers of the form \(N = 2^{\alpha}3^{\beta}M\) where \(\alpha\) is odd, \(\beta\ge\frac{\alpha+1}{2}\) and \(M\) is any positive integer coprime to \(2,3,5\).
</solution>

The proof is rigorous: all necessary conditions are derived from the requirement that the sequence never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. The key lemmas about decreasing orbits for numbers not divisible by 6 are proved, and the recurrence on the quotient \(k_n\) is analysed completely. The characterization of survival is justified by considering the factorisation of \(k=a_1/6\) and the behaviour of the transformation \(k\mapsto 13k/12\) when \(k\) is even, and the odd case with the role of the prime 5. The converse is verified by constructing the sequence and showing it becomes constant at a fixed point. No gaps or unsupported claims remain.
